SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 77 ‚Ä¢ Histology Guide ‚Äì Cardiac Muscle Slide 3.3 Smooth Muscle Tissue : Smooth muscle tissue is one of the three major types of muscle (alongside skeletal and cardiac). It is non - striated , involuntary , and composed of spindle - shaped cells with a single, central nucleus . Unlike skeletal and cardiac muscle, smooth muscle lacks sarcomeres but still contracts using actin and myosin filaments . Tortora & Derrickson : ‚ÄúSmooth muscle tissue is found in the walls of hollow internal structures and is usually activated involuntarily. Its fibers do not exhibit striations under the microscope.‚Äù Figure 4 6 : Smooth muscle tissue. 3.3.1 Characteristics Feature Smooth Muscle Control Involuntary (Autonomic Nervous System, hormones, local factors) Appearance Non - striated, uniform, fusiform (spindle - shaped) cells Nucleus Single, elongated, centrally placed Striations Absent (no visible sarcomeres) Location Found in walls of hollow organs (e.g., GI tract, blood vessels) Contraction Slow, sustained, and energy - efficient Regeneration Good regenerative capacity via mitosis 3.3.2 Microscopic Structure of Smooth Muscle Tissue : Unlike skeletal and cardiac muscle, smooth muscle lacks striations under the light microscope. This is due to the irregular arrangement of actin and myosin filaments, which are not organized into sarcomeres . Despite this, smooth muscle cells are highly specialized and efficient for slow, sustained, and rhythmic contractions . 3.3.3 Microscopic Features of Smooth Muscle Feature Description Cell shape Spindle - shaped (fusiform) : wide in the middle, tapering at both ends Nucleus Single, elongated, centrally located nucleus Striations Absent ‚Äì no sarcomeres, hence uniform appearance Cytoplasm Eosinophilic (pink in H&E), homogeneous without cross - striations Cell arrangement Closely packed , arranged in sheets or bundles; nuclei appear staggered Junctions Gap junctions (unitary type) enable coordinated contractions

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 78 Figure 47 : Microscopic features of smooth muscle tissue in longitudinal section. A . Cell Shape and Arrangement ‚Ä¢ Spindle (fusiform) : The cells are narrow at the ends and thicker in the center, with blunt ends . ‚Ä¢ In longitudinal sections, cells overlap , and their nuclei appear cigar - shaped . ‚Ä¢ In transverse (cross) sections, cells appear small and round , and not all contain visible nuclei (as not every section hits the nucleus). B . Nucleus ‚Ä¢ Each smooth muscle cell contains a single, centrally located nucleus . ‚Ä¢ In a relaxed state , the nucleus appears elongated . ‚Ä¢ During contraction , the nucleus can become twisted or corkscrew - shaped , a classic identifying feature. C . Cytoplasm and Organelles ‚Ä¢ Smooth muscle cytoplasm stains pale pink (eosinophilic) in H&E due to the abundance of actin and myosin . ‚Ä¢ It appears homogenous because contractile proteins are dispersed throughout and not aligned in repeating patterns. ‚Ä¢ Organelles (mitochondria, Golgi, ribosomes) are concentrated near the nucleus. ‚Ä¢ Cells are surrounded by a basal lamina , and often enclosed in endomysium (fine connective tissue layer). D . Contractile Apparatus Smooth muscle lacks sarcomeres , but contraction still occurs via actin ‚Äì myosin interaction, organized differently: Component Function Actin Anchored to dense bodies ; thin filaments Myosin Interacts with actin for contraction; thick filaments

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 79 Dense bodies Analogous to Z - discs; scattered in cytoplasm and near cell membrane Intermediate filaments Provide structural support and transmit contractile force Calmodulin Binds Ca¬≤ ‚Å∫ to activate myosin light chain kinase (MLCK) for contraction Dense bodies act as anchoring sites for actin, allowing force to be transmitted throughout the cell in all directions ‚Äî giving smooth muscle its ability to contract as a whole rather than in linear sarcomere segments. E . Surface Specializations ‚Äì Caveolae ‚Ä¢ Smooth muscle lacks T - tubules , unlike skeletal and cardiac muscle. ‚Ä¢ Instead, the plasma membrane has invaginations called caveolae . ‚Ä¢ These act as calcium entry points and help in signal transduction , functioning similarly to T - tubules but more primitive. F . Gap Junctions (in Unitary Smooth Muscle) ‚Ä¢ Cells in visceral smooth muscle are connected by gap junctions , forming a functional syncytium . ‚Ä¢ Gap junctions allow the spread of ions and electrical impulses , so cells contract as a single unit . ‚Ä¢ Especially important in the GI tract , bladder , and uterus . 3.3.4 Histological Identification Under Light Microscope Section Type Appearance Longitudinal Spindle - shaped cells with elongated nuclei; no striations Cross - section Circular or oval profiles; some cells may lack visible nuclei (due to sectioning) Staining Cytoplasm is pink; nucleus stains basophilic (blue - purple) in H&E Microscopic appearance is generally more uniform and less organized than skeletal or cardiac muscle. Summary Table: Microscopic Features of Smooth Muscle Feature Smooth Muscle Cell shape Spindle - shaped, overlapping Nucleus Single, central, elongated; corkscrew during contraction Striations Absent Sarcomeres Absent Actin & Myosin Present, irregularly arranged Dense bodies Replace Z - lines; anchor actin filaments Intermediate filaments Crisscross the cytoplasm T - tubules Absent Caveolae Present; involved in calcium signaling Intercellular junctions Gap junctions (in unitary type) Regeneration Present; smooth muscle cells can divide 3.3.5 Organization of Smooth Muscle in Tissues : Smooth muscle tissue is organized in distinct architectural patterns within the organs it serves, depending on function ,

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 80 direction of contraction , and control mechanisms . Unlike skeletal muscle, which is organized in bundles of parallel fibers attached to bones, smooth muscle is typically organized in sheets, layers, or bundles within the walls of hollow organs . Figure 48 : Organization of smooth muscle in tissues . ‚û¢ Concepts of Organization ‚Ä¢ Smooth muscle is not arranged into fascicles like skeletal muscle . ‚Ä¢ Its arrangement depends on the mechanical function of the organ. ‚Ä¢ It may be organized in circular , longitudinal , oblique , or even spiral layers to coordinate complex movements like peristalsis, sphincter closure, or uterine contractions. ‚û¢ Layered Arrangements in Hollow Organs The most common and functionally significant arrangement is the dual - layered structure : A. Inner Circular Layer ‚Ä¢ Muscle fibers are arranged in a circular manner around the lumen of the organ. ‚Ä¢ When these fibers contract , they narrow the lumen of the organ. ‚Ä¢ Found in: o Esophagus, intestines (peristalsis) o Blood vessels (vasoconstriction) o Bronchi (bronchoconstriction) o Iris of the eye (pupil constriction) Function : Decreases lumen diameter, important for propulsion and restriction of flow . B . Outer Longitudinal Layer ‚Ä¢ Fibers run along the length of the organ. ‚Ä¢ When these fibers contract , they shorten the organ .

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 81 ‚Ä¢ Found in: o GI tract , helping to propel contents forward o Uterus , assisting with expulsion during labor o Urinary bladder , aiding in micturition Function : Shortens the tube or organ, producing wave - like contractions for movement. C . Additional Oblique or Spiral Layers (in some organs) ‚Ä¢ Present in organs that require multi - directional movement or force . ‚Ä¢ Example: Stomach o Has three layers : inner oblique, middle circular, outer longitudinal. o This enhances the churning and mixing of food with digestive enzymes. Example: GI Tract Wall Layer Smooth Muscle Arrangement Function Mucosa (innermost) ‚Äî Secretion and absorption Muscularis mucosae Thin circular smooth muscle Maintains mucosal tone Muscularis externa Inner circular + outer longitudinal Peristalsis and segmentation Serosa/Adventitia ‚Äî Outer connective tissue Between the two layers of the muscularis externa lies the myenteric (Auerbach‚Äôs) plexus , part of the enteric nervous system , which controls smooth muscle contractions. ‚û¢ Functional Importance of Layering Organ Layer Arrangement Function Enabled Esophagus Circular + longitudinal Propel food toward stomach via peristalsis Intestines Circular + longitudinal Mix and move contents along tract Uterus Circular + longitudinal (dense meshwork) Strong, multidirectional contractions during childbirth Urinary bladder Interwoven network Uniform contraction to expel urine Stomach Oblique + circular + longitudinal Churning and mixing food with enzymes Blood vessels Circular only (mainly) Control of vessel diameter (blood pressure regulation) Iris of eye Radial and circular layers Pupil dilation and constriction ‚û¢ Microscopy of Smooth Muscle Organization A. In Longitudinal Section: ‚Ä¢ Muscle cells appear long and fusiform , with elongated nuclei . ‚Ä¢ Layers are seen running in a single direction (depending on the organ section). B. In Cross - section: ‚Ä¢ Cells appear circular or oval , with or without visible nuclei depending on the cut. ‚Ä¢ Multiple layers appear stacked concentrically in tubular organs.

C. Integration with Nervous and Vascular Systems Smooth muscle layers are richly supplied by: ‚Ä¢ Autonomic nerves (sympathetic and parasympathetic) ‚Ä¢ Enteric nervous system (especially in GI tract) ‚Ä¢ Capillary networks , embedded in the surrounding connective tissue (endomysium) References ‚Ä¢ Tortora & Derrickson, Principles of Anatomy & Physiology , Chapter 10 ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson, Anatomy and Physiology in Health and Illness ‚Ä¢ Kenhub ‚Äì Smooth Muscle Tissue ‚Ä¢ Histology Guide ‚Äì Smooth Muscle ‚Ä¢ OpenStax, A&P , Muscle Tissue Section Summary Table: Organization of Smooth Muscle Layer Type Orientation Effect of Contraction Inner circular Around the lumen Constriction ‚Äì narrows the passage Outer longitudinal Along the tube Shortening ‚Äì pushes content forward Oblique/spiral Variable (if present) Mixing and twisting (e.g., stomach, bladder) 3.3.6 Types of Smooth Muscle Tissue : Smooth muscle tissue, although uniform in microscopic appearance, is functionally and physiologically classified into two major types based on how the muscle cells are innervated , contract , and communicate with each other : A . Unitary (Visceral) Smooth Muscle ‚Äì Also known as single - unit smooth muscle Figure 49 : Structure of unitary (visceral) smooth muscle. Definition : Unitary smooth muscle is made of sheets or bundles of muscle fibers that function as a single coordinated unit . The cells are electrically connected via gap

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 83 junctions , allowing the entire muscle mass to contract simultaneously , much like cardiac muscle. ‚û¢ Microscopic Features Feature Description Gap junctions Present ‚Äî enable electrical impulse transmission between cells Nerve endings Sparse ‚Äî one impulse can stimulate multiple cells Arrangement Often in sheets or layers Pacemaker cells Present in some tissues; initiate spontaneous activity ‚û¢ Contraction Mechanism ‚Ä¢ Begins from one or a few pacemaker cells ‚Ä¢ Depolarization spreads via gap junctions ‚Ä¢ Slow - wave potentials or spontaneous depolarizations may occur ‚Ä¢ Can contract without nervous stimulation ‚û¢ Locations Organ/System Role of Unitary Smooth Muscle Gastrointestinal tract Produces peristalsis , segmentation, mixing Uterus Coordinated contractions during labor Urinary bladder Expels urine via synchronized wall contraction Small blood vessels Maintains vascular tone via myogenic activity ‚û¢ Functional Characteristics ‚Ä¢ Auto - rhythmic or myogenic ‚Ä¢ Modulated by: o Autonomic nervous system (ANS) o Hormones (e.g., oxytocin in uterus) o Stretch (e.g., bladder wall distension) ‚Ä¢ Responds slowly but sustainedly ‚Ä¢ Fatigue - resistant ‚û¢ Clinical Insight ‚Ä¢ Labor contractions in the uterus occur due to oxytocin - stimulated unitary smooth muscle ‚Ä¢ IBS (Irritable Bowel Syndrome) may involve abnormal visceral smooth muscle activity B . Multi - Unit Smooth Muscle : Multi - unit smooth muscle consists of independent smooth muscle fibers , each with its own motor neuron innervation . There are few or no gap junctions , so each cell contracts independently of its neighbor.

appears corkscrew - shaped when contracted Striations Absent Cell borders Not clearly visible without special stains Arrangement In layers (longitudinal + circular); wavy appearance Visual references: ‚Ä¢ Histology Guide ‚Äì Smooth Muscle ‚Ä¢ Kenhub ‚Äì Smooth Muscle Tissue 3.3.9 Functions o f Smooth Muscle Function Examples Peristalsis Movement of food in the digestive tract Vasoconstriction/Dilation Regulation of blood pressure via contraction of arteries Bronchoconstriction In respiratory airways (e.g., asthma) Urination & Micturition Contraction of bladder wall Labor contractions In uterus during childbirth Pupil control Iris muscles adjust light entry Piloerection Arrector pili muscle causes "goosebumps" References ‚Ä¢ Tortora & Derrickson , Principles of Anatomy & Physiology , Chapter 10

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 86 ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson , Anatomy and Physiology in Health & Illness ‚Ä¢ Kenhub ‚Äì Smooth Muscle Tissue ‚Ä¢ OpenStax ‚Äì Muscle Tissues ‚Ä¢ Histology Guide ‚Äì Smooth Muscle Slide 4 Nervous Tissue : Nervous tissue is one of the four fundamental tissue types in the body, alongside epithelial , connective , and muscular tissues. It is the main tissue of the nervous system , which includes the brain , spinal cord , and peripheral nerves . Nervous tissue is highly specialized for: ‚Ä¢ Communication ‚Ä¢ Integration ‚Ä¢ Coordination of bodily functions It accomplishes this through two main types of cells: a) Neurons ‚Äì the excitable, signal - transmitting cells b) Neuroglia (Glial cells) ‚Äì the supporting, insulating, and protective cells Neurons send and receive signals; neuroglia take care of them. a) Neurons : Neurons , also known as nerve cells , are the fundamental units of the nervous system . They are specialized for the generation , conduction , and transmission of electrical and chemical signals throughout the body. Neurons serve three major roles: ‚Ä¢ Receiving stimuli from internal and external environments ‚Ä¢ Processing (integrating) the information ‚Ä¢ Sending signals to effectors (muscles, glands, or other neurons) These cells form complex neural networks that underlie everything from reflexes to thoughts. ‚û¢ Histogenesis (Origin of Neurons) ‚Ä¢ Embryologically, neurons are derived from the ectoderm , specifically from the neural tube and neural crest . ‚Ä¢ Neuroblasts differentiate into mature neurons, which lose their ability to divide after development (except in specific brain regions like the hippocampus). ‚û¢ Structure of a Neuron : Neurons are highly polarized , meaning their structures are functionally and structurally distinct .

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 87 A typical neuron has the following components: i . Cell Body (Soma / Perikaryon) Description : ‚Ä¢ The central metabolic hub of the neuron. ‚Ä¢ Contains: o A large, pale nucleus with a prominent nucleolus (for high RNA synthesis) o Nissl bodies ‚Äì stacks of rough endoplasmic reticulum (RER) rich in ribosomes o Golgi apparatus, mitochondria, lysosomes, and other organelles Function : ‚Ä¢ Synthesizes neurotransmitters and proteins ‚Ä¢ Maintains cellular integrity ‚Ä¢ Integrates incoming signals (with dendrites) Nissl bodies are unique to neurons and are responsible for the synthesis of neurotransmitters. ii . Dendrites Description : ‚Ä¢ Short, tapering, branched extensions from the soma ‚Ä¢ Often covered with dendritic spines (sites of synaptic input) ‚Ä¢ Typically unmyelinated Function : ‚Ä¢ Receive impulses from other neurons or sensory receptors ‚Ä¢ Transmit these graded potentials toward the soma A single neuron may have thousands of synapses on its dendrites, enabling complex integration of information. iii . Axon (Nerve Fiber) Description : ‚Ä¢ A long, cylindrical projection from the axon hillock ‚Ä¢ One axon per neuron , but it may branch (axon collaterals) ‚Ä¢ Ends in axon terminals or synaptic boutons Function :

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 88 ‚Ä¢ Conducts action potentials away from the cell body toward other neurons or effectors Features : ‚Ä¢ Axon hillock : Cone - shaped base where action potential initiates ‚Ä¢ Initial segment : Most excitable part; site of impulse generation ‚Ä¢ Myelin sheath : Insulating layer that increases conduction speed ‚Ä¢ Nodes of Ranvier : Gaps in myelin that allow saltatory conduction Myelinated axons transmit impulses 100x faster than unmyelinated ones. iv . Axon Terminals (Telodendria / Synaptic End Bulbs) Description : ‚Ä¢ Distal endings of axon collaterals ‚Ä¢ Swollen tips called synaptic boutons ‚Ä¢ Contain synaptic vesicles filled with neurotransmitters (e.g., acetylcholine, dopamine) Function : ‚Ä¢ Transmit signals across the synaptic cleft to another neuron, muscle, or gland ‚Ä¢ Convert electrical signal ‚Üí chemical signal Neuron Structure Figure 51: Structure of Neuron ‚û¢ Functional Properties of Neurons Property Description

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 89 Excitability Ability to respond to stimuli by generating electrical signals (graded/action potentials) Conductivity Ability to propagate electrical signals over long distances (via axon) Secretion Ability to release neurotransmitters to communicate with target cells Longevity Neurons can survive a lifetime (many > 100 years) Amitotic Most neurons do not divide (exceptions in hippocampus, olfactory bulb) High metabolic rate Require continuous supply of oxygen and glucose ‚û¢ Classification o f Neurons Figure 52 : Classification of neurons. i. By Structure Type Features Examples Multipolar Many dendrites, one axon Motor neurons, CNS interneurons Bipolar One dendrite, one axon Retina, olfactory bulb Unipolar Single process splits into axon & dendrite Dorsal root ganglia (sensory) Anaxonic No axon; only dendrites Certain interneurons in CNS ii. By Function Type Function Example Sensory (Afferent) Carry info from body to CNS Receptors in skin, eyes Motor (Efferent) Carry commands from CNS to effectors Skeletal muscle motor neurons Interneurons Process and relay signals within CNS Cortical and spinal cord neurons Interneurons make up 99% of all neurons! b) Neuroglia (Glial Cells) : Neuroglia , or glial cells , are non - excitable support cells in the nervous system. Though they do not generate nerve impulses , they are vital to the function, development, and maintenance of neurons . ‚Ä¢ Glial cells are like the nanny cells ‚Äî they feed, clean, protect, insulate, and guide the neurons.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 90 ‚Ä¢ Glial cells make up about 50 ‚Äì 90% of all cells in the central nervous system (CNS) and are 5 ‚Äì 25 times more numerous than neurons. Historical Note : The word ‚Äúglia‚Äù is derived from the Greek word for ‚Äúglue,‚Äù reflecting early beliefs that these cells simply held neurons together. Today, we understand they perform many more functions , including regulating synapses, myelination, homeostasis , and immune defense . Figure 53 : Neuroglial cells and functions. ‚û¢ Types Of Neuroglia There are six major types of glial cells: ‚Ä¢ Four in the CNS ‚Ä¢ Two in the PNS ‚û¢ Neuroglia In the Central Nervous System (CNS) (Brain and spinal cord) a) Astrocytes Structure : ‚Ä¢ Star - shaped cells with many cytoplasmic processes ‚Ä¢ Most abundant glial cells in the CNS Types : ‚Ä¢ Protoplasmic astrocytes ‚Äì found in gray matter ‚Ä¢ Fibrous astrocytes ‚Äì found in white matter Functions : ‚Ä¢ Form the blood - brain barrier (BBB) by wrapping around capillaries ‚Ä¢ Regulate ion concentrations (especially K ‚Å∫ ) in the extracellular space ‚Ä¢ Participate in neurotransmitter uptake and recycling (e.g., glutamate) ‚Ä¢ Provide metabolic support (glucose, lactate) to neurons ‚Ä¢ Involved in repair and scar formation after injury (gliosis)

Introduction to the Human Body : Human Anatomy and Physiology form the foundation of biomedical and life sciences . Every clinical or therapeutic decision ‚Äî from surgical interventions to drug action ‚Äî rests on a solid understanding of these subjects. ‚Ä¢ Anatomy answers: ‚ÄúWhat is the structure?‚Äù ‚Ä¢ Physiology answers: ‚ÄúHow does it function?‚Äù Figure 1: Branches of anatomy and physiology.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 2 Definition of Anatomy: Anatomy is the branch of biology concerned with the study of the structure of organisms and their parts , especially the human body. From the textbooks: ‚Ä¢ Tortora & Derrickson (Ch. 1) : "Anatomy is the science of body structures and the relationships among them." ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson : ‚ÄúAnatomy explores how the human body is built, while maintaining a relationship with its functions.‚Äù ‚Ä¢ BD Chaurasia : "Anatomy means cutting up ( dissection ) and refers to the study of internal body parts through various methods including cadaveric study, microscopy, and imaging." Types (Branches) of Anatomy: Each branch gives us a unique view of body structure: Type of Anatomy Description Examples / Applications Gross Anatomy Study of structures visible to the naked eye Muscles, bones, organs Regional Anatomy Body studied by areas or regions Head, chest, abdomen Systemic Anatomy Study of specific organ systems Skeletal, nervous, digestive systems Microscopic Anatomy Structures seen only under a microscope Cells (cytology), Tissues (histology) Developmental Anatomy Study of structural changes from fertilization to adulthood Embryology, fetal development Surface Anatomy Study of body landmarks on the skin surface Pulse points, bony prominences Radiographic Anatomy Structures seen using imaging methods X - ray, CT, MRI, ultrasound Clinical/Applied Anatomy Application of anatomical knowledge to clinical practice Surgery, diagnosis, procedures Comparative Anatomy Study of anatomy across species Research, evolution studies Living Anatomy Study on living subjects using inspection, palpation, auscultation, and imaging Bedside examination, physiotherapy Ross & Wilson emphasizes the integration of anatomy with clinical relevance ‚Äî especially through surface anatomy, imaging, and age - related anatomical changes. Definition of Physiology: Physiology is the scientific study of normal functions in living systems ‚Äî how various body parts work individually and together to sustain life. From the textbooks: ‚Ä¢ Tortora (Ch. 1) : "Physiology is the science of body functions ‚Äî how the body parts work." ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson : "Physiology describes how the body maintains homeostasis and responds to challenges internally and externally."

If structure is altered, function is often impaired." Example : ‚Ä¢ The alveoli in lungs are thin - walled and numerous, structurally optimized for gas exchange . ‚Ä¢ If the alveolar walls thicken (as in fibrosis), gas exchange is reduced ‚Äî a physiological impairment due to structural change . Tortora explains this as the ‚Äúprinciple of complementarity‚Äù . Scope of Anatomy and Physiology The scope is vast and essential across health sciences: a) In Medical Sciences: ‚Ä¢ Diagnostics : Identifying abnormalities (e.g., X - rays in fractures) ‚Ä¢ Surgical planning : Precise knowledge of anatomical relations ‚Ä¢ Pharmacology : Understanding drug actions and physiological effects b) In Nursing and Allied Health: ‚Ä¢ Vital signs monitoring : Tied to cardiac and respiratory physiology ‚Ä¢ Patient assessment : Palpation, auscultation, neurological checks ‚Ä¢ Therapeutics : Physiotherapy, nutrition, rehabilitation c) In Research and Industry: ‚Ä¢ Medical imaging and device design ‚Ä¢ Pharmaceutical testing ‚Ä¢ Biotechnology and AI in healthcare 7 . Why Study Anatomy and Physiology? a) Builds the foundation for all medical and pharmaceutical sciences. b) Prepares for clinical decision - making . c) Essential for diagnosis, therapy, patient education , and public health .

Levels of Structural Organization in Human Anatomy and Physiology Introduction : Understanding the hierarchical structure of the human body ‚Äî from atoms to the complete organism ‚Äî is essential for grasping how our bodies function. This framework is called the levels of structural organization , and it reflects how simple chemical units progressively combine to form complex living systems. Each level contributes to the form and function of the next level above it, resulting in a highly integrated, functional human body . Figure 2 : Pyramid diagram of levels of structural organization in the human body. As Tortora states: ‚ÄúThe human body consists of several levels of structural organization that are built one upon another, from the simplest chemical level to the most complex organismal level.‚Äù Principles of Anatomy and Physiology (Tortora, Chapter 1) a) Chemical Level ‚Äì The Simplest Level Definition: This is the foundation of all matter , including the human body. It consists of: ‚Ä¢ Atoms : The smallest units of matter (e.g., Carbon, Hydrogen, Oxygen, Nitrogen) ‚Ä¢ Molecules : Formed when atoms bond together (e.g., Water ‚Äì H ‚ÇÇ O, Glucose ‚Äì C ‚ÇÜ H ‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇ O ‚ÇÜ , DNA) Significance: ‚Ä¢ Molecules such as proteins, lipids, carbohydrates, and nucleic acids form the structural and functional basis of cells. ‚Ä¢ Enzymes , made from proteins, regulate nearly all chemical reactions.

All physiological functions occur at the cellular level. Features: ‚Ä¢ Cells contain organelles (e.g., nucleus, mitochondria, ribosomes). ‚Ä¢ Different cells perform specialized functions (e.g., muscle cells contract; nerve cells transmit impulses). Examples: ‚Ä¢ RBCs (Red Blood Cells): Carry oxygen ‚Ä¢ Neurons : Transmit signals ‚Ä¢ Myocytes : Facilitate movement As BD Chaurasia explains: ‚ÄúA cell is a living structural and functional unit of all living organisms.‚Äù BD Chaurasia‚Äôs Handbook of General Anatomy, Ch.1 c) Tissue Level ‚Äì Similar Cells Working Together Definition: A tissue is a group of similar cells that function together to perform a particular task. Major Types: Type Function Example Epithelial Covers and protects body surfaces Skin, lining of intestines Connective Supports, binds, and protects organs Bone, blood, fat tissue Muscular Facilitates movement Skeletal, cardiac muscles Nervous Transmits electrical impulses Brain, spinal cord, nerves Ross and Wilson classify tissues as ‚Äúspecialized groups of cells and intercellular material that perform particular functions.‚Äù Ross & Wilson, Ch.3 d) Organ Level ‚Äì Structures with Specific Functions Definition: An organ is a structure composed of two or more different types of tissues organized to perform specific functions. Characteristics: ‚Ä¢ Most organs contain all four tissue types . ‚Ä¢ They are identifiable shapes and functions. Examples:

Examples of Organ Systems: System Main Organs Function Digestive Mouth, stomach, intestines Digestion & nutrient absorption Respiratory Nose, lungs, trachea Gas exchange (O ‚ÇÇ /CO ‚ÇÇ ) Cardiovascular Heart, blood vessels Transport of blood and nutrients Nervous Brain, spinal cord, nerves Communication and coordination Musculoskeletal Muscles, bones Movement and support According to Ross and Wilson , ‚ÄúEach system contributes to the overall function of the human body and is interdependent on others.‚Äù Ross & Wilson, Ch.1 f) Organism Level ‚Äì The Complete Living Person Definition: The organism level is the highest level of organization , representing a living individual . Features: ‚Ä¢ Made up of all organ systems working in harmony . ‚Ä¢ Capable of performing all basic life processes: metabolism, responsiveness, movement, growth, reproduction, and differentiation. SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 12 Figure 4 : The six basic life processes essential for human survival. As defined in Tortora & Derrickson , ‚ÄúLife processes are characteristics that distinguish living organisms from nonliving things.‚Äù Principles of Anatomy & Physiology, Chapter 1 Each of these processes is interconnected , relying on the structural organization of the body and functioning in harmony to maintain homeostasis (internal stability). a) Metabolism Definition : The sum total of all chemical reactions in the body. It includes two main types: ‚Ä¢ Catabolism : Breaking down larger molecules into simpler ones (e.g., glucose ‚Üí ATP) ‚Ä¢ Anabolism : Building complex molecules from simpler ones (e.g., amino acids ‚Üí proteins) Functions : ‚Ä¢ Provides energy (ATP) for body functions ‚Ä¢ Supports growth, repair, and maintenance Example : Digestion of carbohydrates produces glucose ‚Üí enters cells ‚Üí converted into ATP Ross & Wilson : ‚ÄúMetabolism includes both constructive and destructive processes which maintain body activity.‚Äù b) Responsiveness Definition : The body‚Äôs ability to detect and respond to changes in its internal and external environments. Functions : ‚Ä¢ Protects against injury or harm ‚Ä¢ Maintains internal balance by adjusting to stimuli Example :

Types : ‚Ä¢ External movement : Walking, running (skeletal muscle activity) ‚Ä¢ Internal movement : Movement of blood, food, urine, and lymph ‚Ä¢ Cellular movement : White blood cells migrating to infection sites Function : Essential for locomotion, digestion, circulation, respiration, and reflexes Tortora explains movement as one of the key physiological properties of living beings. d) Growth Definition : An increase in size and complexity due to an increase in: ‚Ä¢ Cell number (hyperplasia) ‚Ä¢ Cell size (hypertrophy) ‚Ä¢ Matrix between cells (e.g., bone mineral) Functions : ‚Ä¢ Supports tissue expansion and repair ‚Ä¢ Essential in development and healing Example : ‚Ä¢ Growth of a child ‚Ä¢ Muscle growth with exercise ‚Ä¢ Healing after a wound Tortora : ‚ÄúGrowth involves an increase in body size due to an increase in cells or materials between cells.‚Äù e) Differentiation Definition : The process through which unspecialized cells become specialized to perform distinct functions.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 15 References ‚Ä¢ Tortora & Derrickson , Principles of Anatomy and Physiology, Chapter 1 ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson , Anatomy and Physiology in Health and Illness, 12th Ed ‚Ä¢ BD Chaurasia , Handbook of General Anatomy, Ch.1 ‚Ä¢ OpenStax A&P ‚Äì Functions of Human Life ‚Ä¢ LibreTexts ‚Äì Functions of Human Life Homeostasis in Human Anatomy and Physiology Introduction : The human body is constantly exposed to internal and external changes ‚Äî ranging from temperature fluctuations to chemical imbalances. To survive and function efficiently , it must maintain internal constancy . This self - regulating mechanism is termed homeostasis . Origin of the Term: ‚Ä¢ Coined by Walter Cannon in 1926, from the Greek homeo - (similar) and - stasis (standing still). ‚Ä¢ It builds upon Claude Bernard‚Äôs concept of the milieu int√©rieur (internal environment), which emphasizes a stable internal condition essential for life. Tortora & Derrickson (Ch. 1) define homeostasis as: ‚ÄúThe condition of equilibrium in the body‚Äôs internal environment due to the constant interaction of the body‚Äôs many regulatory processes.‚Äù Core Concept of Homeostasis : Homeostasis involves maintaining physical and chemical parameters (called set points ) within narrow physiological limits . Examples include: ‚Ä¢ Body temperature ‚âà 37 ¬∞ C ‚Ä¢ Blood glucose ‚âà 90 ‚Äì 110 mg/dL ‚Ä¢ Blood pH ‚âà 7.35 ‚Äì 7.45 ‚Ä¢ Blood pressure ‚âà 120/80 mmHg Feedback Mechanisms: The Foundation of Homeostatic Control Components of a Feedback System A typical feedback loop includes: a) Receptor (Sensor) ‚Äì Detects a change in a variable (stimulus)

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 16 b) Control Center ‚Äì Usually the brain/hypothalamus; interprets input and sends out commands c) Effector ‚Äì Performs the response that restores balance Ross & Wilson explains these as: ‚Äúbiological control systems that monitor conditions and initiate corrective actions.‚Äù Types o f Feedback Mechanisms a) Negative Feedback Definition : A mechanism that reverses a deviation from the set point. It is the most common feedback mechanism. Goal : Restore equilibrium by counteracting the change . Examples : ‚Ä¢ Thermoregulation : o Stimulus : Rise in body temperature o Receptors : Thermoreceptors in skin o Control Center : Hypothalamus o Effectors : Sweat glands (increase perspiration); blood vessels (vasodilation) o Response : Cooling of the body ‚û° Feedback loop reverses the deviation and shuts off when balance is restored. ‚Ä¢ Blood Glucose Regulation : o Stimulus : Rise in blood sugar after a meal o Receptors : Beta cells of pancreas o Control Center : Pancreas o Effectors : Insulin release ‚Üí cells absorb glucose o Response : Blood glucose lowers to normal level BD Chaurasia emphasizes negative feedback as ‚Äúa protective mechanism which defends normal physiological state.‚Äù b) Positive Feedback Definition : A mechanism that amplifies the original stimulus , moving the system away from its set point until a specific outcome is achieved. Goal : Intensify the change to complete a process , then shut off. Examples :

It operates only in limited conditions requiring a definite outcome.‚Äù FEEDBACK LOOP DIAGRAMS Negative Feedback Loop (Thermoregulation) Figure 5 : Negative feedback loop maintaining body temperature homeostasis. Positive Feedback Loop (Childbirth) Figure 6 : Positive feedback loop (Childbirth) .

VeryWellHealth ‚Äì Homeostasis Basic Anatomical Terminology Introduction : In human anatomy and physiology, accurate description of body location, orientation, and structure is essential. For this, anatomists use standardized terms that eliminate ambiguity regardless of body position. This framework is called Basic Anatomical Terminology , and includes: a) Anatomical Position b) Directional Terms c) Body Planes and Sections

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 19 d) Body Cavities As per Tortora & Derrickson (Ch. 1) : ‚ÄúStandard anatomical terminology provides clarity in describing body structures and their relationships.‚Äù a) Anatomical Position Definition: The standard reference posture used in anatomical descriptions. Characteristics: ‚Ä¢ Body standing upright ‚Ä¢ Head level , eyes looking forward ‚Ä¢ Arms at the sides , palms facing forward ‚Ä¢ Feet flat on the floor, facing forward Ross & Wilson note: ‚ÄúAll directional terms refer to the body in the anatomical position, regardless of its actual posture.‚Äù b) Directional Terms Used to describe location and orientation of one structure relative to another. Term Meaning Example Superior (Cranial) Toward the head The brain is superior to the heart Inferior (Caudal) Toward the feet The bladder is inferior to the stomach Anterior (Ventral) Toward the front The sternum is anterior to the heart Posterior (Dorsal) Toward the back The spine is posterior to the lungs Medial Toward the midline The nose is medial to the eyes Lateral Away from the midline The lungs are lateral to the heart Proximal Closer to the origin or trunk The elbow is proximal to the wrist Distal Farther from the origin or trunk The fingers are distal to the shoulder Superficial Near the surface The skin is superficial to muscles Deep Away from the surface The kidneys are deep to the abdominal wall Tortora highlights: ‚ÄúThese terms provide precise and consistent descriptions of body structure.‚Äù Figure 7 : Anatomical position and commonly used directional terms.

Plane Description Visual Orientation Sagittal Plane Divides body into left and right parts Vertical cut from front to back Midsagittal (Median) Equal left and right halves Direct midline vertical cut Parasagittal Unequal left and right Off - center sagittal cut Frontal (Coronal) Divides into anterior and posterior Vertical cut side - to - side Transverse (Horizontal) Divides into superior and inferior Horizontal cut (top and bottom) Oblique Plane At an angle between horizontal and vertical Diagonal cross - section Ross & Wilson state: ‚ÄúThese planes are fundamental in cross - sectional imaging like CT or MRI.‚Äù Figure 8 : Anatomical planes and sections. d) Body Cavities Body cavities are spaces within the body that: ‚Ä¢ House and protect internal organs ‚Ä¢ Allow organ movement and expansion ‚Ä¢ Often contain serous membranes that reduce friction Figure 9 : Major body cavities.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 22 C ellular Level of Organization I . Cell Structure and Functions : The cell is the structural and functional unit of life . All organisms, from unicellular bacteria to complex multicellular humans, are composed of cells. The human body contains approximately 37 trillion cells , all varying in shape, size, and function ‚Äî but all sharing common fundamental structures. Understanding cell structure and organelle function is foundational to anatomy and physiology. From energy production to protein synthesis and genetic control, the cell is the biological hub of all life processes . Tortora & Derrickson (Chapter 3) state: ‚ÄúThe cell is the smallest unit capable of performing life processes.‚Äù 1 . Structure of a Typical Eukaryotic Cell Figure 1 0 : Structure of an animal cell. a) Plasma Membrane Figure 1 1 : Plasma membrane components.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 23 Structure: ‚Ä¢ Phospholipid bilayer : Composed of amphipathic phospholipids (hydrophilic heads & hydrophobic tails). ‚Ä¢ Contains cholesterol , which stabilizes membrane fluidity. ‚Ä¢ Embedded with integral and peripheral proteins : o Integral proteins: span the membrane (e.g., channels, carriers, receptors). o Peripheral proteins: attached to membrane surfaces. Molecular Functions: ‚Ä¢ Selective permeability : Allows specific molecules to pass via: o Passive diffusion (O ‚ÇÇ , CO ‚ÇÇ ), facilitated diffusion (glucose), active transport (Na ‚Å∫ /K ‚Å∫ pump). ‚Ä¢ Signal transduction : Receptors bind ligands (e.g., hormones), activating intracellular cascades. ‚Ä¢ Cell adhesion molecules (CAMs) : Help cells stick to each other or ECM. Clinical Relevance: Defects in membrane proteins can lead to diseases like cystic fibrosis (CFTR gene mutation affecting chloride channels). b) Cytoplasm Structure: ‚Ä¢ Cytosol : Semi - fluid medium composed of water, ions (Na ‚Å∫ , K ‚Å∫ , Cl ‚Åª ), enzymes, nutrients, and cytoskeletal elements. ‚Ä¢ Suspends organelles . Functions: ‚Ä¢ Site for glycolysis and protein translation (free ribosomes). ‚Ä¢ Maintains osmotic balance and pH buffering . ‚Ä¢ Supports vesicle trafficking and organelle mobility . c) Nucleus Figure 1 2 : Nucleus structure.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 24 Structure: ‚Ä¢ Double nuclear envelope : Continuous with rough ER, contains nuclear pores for regulated exchange. ‚Ä¢ Contains chromatin : o Euchromatin : loosely packed, transcriptionally active. o Heterochromatin : densely packed, transcriptionally silent. ‚Ä¢ Nucleolus : Dense, non - membranous structure that synthesizes rRNA and assembles ribosomes. Functions: ‚Ä¢ Stores genetic material ( DNA ) organized into chromosomes. ‚Ä¢ Coordinates transcription , RNA processing , and DNA replication . ‚Ä¢ Nucleoplasm : Suspends nucleic acids and enzymes. Clinical Note: Mutations in nuclear envelope proteins (e.g., laminopathies ) cause progeria or muscular dystrophies. d) Ribosomes Structure: ‚Ä¢ Made of rRNA + ribosomal proteins . ‚Ä¢ Two subunits: 60S (large) and 40S (small) in eukaryotes. ‚Ä¢ Found free in cytosol or attached to RER . Functions: ‚Ä¢ Sites of protein synthesis (translation of mRNA ‚Üí polypeptides). ‚Ä¢ Free ribosomes ‚Üí cytoplasmic proteins. ‚Ä¢ Bound ribosomes ‚Üí secreted, membrane, or lysosomal proteins. e) Rough Endoplasmic Reticulum (RER) Figure 1 3 : Endoplasmic reticulum types.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 25 Structure: ‚Ä¢ Series of flattened sacs with attached ribosomes . ‚Ä¢ Continuous with outer nuclear membrane . Functions: ‚Ä¢ Synthesizes membrane - bound and secretory proteins . ‚Ä¢ Assists in protein folding via chaperones. ‚Ä¢ Adds initial post - translational modifications (glycosylation). ‚Ä¢ Sends proteins to the Golgi via vesicles. Pathology: Protein misfolding in the ER can lead to ER stress and diseases like Alzheimer‚Äôs . f) Smooth Endoplasmic Reticulum (SER) Structure: Interconnected tubules lacking ribosomes. Functions: ‚Ä¢ Lipid and steroid hormone synthesis (abundant in adrenal cortex, testes). ‚Ä¢ Detoxification of drugs and poisons (cytochrome P450 enzymes in hepatocytes). ‚Ä¢ Calcium storage and release in muscle cells (sarcoplasmic reticulum). ‚Ä¢ Synthesizes membrane lipids . g) Golgi Apparatus Figure 14: Golgi apparatus. Structure: Stacks of flattened cisternae with a cis face (receives from ER) and trans face (sends vesicles). Functions: ‚Ä¢ Modifies proteins/lipids from ER (e.g., glycosylation, sulfation). ‚Ä¢ Sorts and packages proteins into vesicles: o Secretory vesicles ‚Üí exocytosis. o Lysosomes ‚Üí digestion.

Functions:

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 28 ‚Ä¢ Microfilaments (Actin) : cell shape, movement (e.g., in muscle cells). ‚Ä¢ Intermediate filaments : mechanical stability (e.g., keratin). ‚Ä¢ Microtubules (Tubulin) : o Form centrioles , cilia , and flagella . o Highways for vesicle/organelle transport. Functions: ‚Ä¢ Cilia : Move mucus and particles over epithelial surfaces. ‚Ä¢ Flagella : Propel sperm cell for motility. o) Vesicles and Vacuoles Structure: Membrane - bound sacs. Functions: ‚Ä¢ Transport materials between organelles or to/from plasma membrane. ‚Ä¢ Endocytosis : engulf material into the cell. ‚Ä¢ Exocytosis : expel substances out of the cell.

This regulation is vital for: ‚Ä¢ Maintaining cellular homeostasis ‚Ä¢ Controlling osmotic balance ‚Ä¢ Nutrient uptake and waste removal ‚Ä¢ Intercellular signaling Transport across the membrane can occur with or without energy , and is broadly classified into: ‚Ä¢ Passive Transport (does not require energy) ‚Ä¢ Active Transport (requires ATP) Tortora & Derrickson describe the plasma membrane as ‚Äúa dynamic interface that mediates cell - environment interactions and regulates molecular traffic.‚Äù

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 30 I. Membrane Transport Types Transport Type Energy Used Direction (Concentration Gradient) Example Substances Passive Transport No High ‚Üí Low O ‚ÇÇ , CO ‚ÇÇ , water, glucose Active Transport Yes Low ‚Üí High Na ‚Å∫ , K ‚Å∫ , H ‚Å∫ , large molecules Vesicular Transport Yes Bulk in/out Hormones, bacteria, fluids 1 . Passive Transport Does not require ATP Depends on concentration gradients a) Simple Diffusion: Movement of small, non - polar molecules directly across the lipid bilayer from high to low concentration . Examples: ‚Ä¢ O ‚ÇÇ and CO ‚ÇÇ exchange in alveoli ‚Ä¢ Movement of steroid hormones Factors influencing diffusion: ‚Ä¢ Concentration gradient ‚Ä¢ Membrane permeability ‚Ä¢ Temperature ‚Ä¢ Surface area Ross & Wilson : ‚ÄúDiffusion is the primary mechanism of gas exchange in the body.‚Äù b) Facilitated Diffusion: Movement of larger or charged molecules via transport proteins , down the concentration gradient. Types: ‚Ä¢ Channel - mediated : Ion channels (e.g., Na ‚Å∫ , K ‚Å∫ , Cl ‚Åª ) ‚Ä¢ Carrier - mediated : Glucose transporters (e.g., GLUT - 1 in RBCs) Examples: ‚Ä¢ Entry of glucose into cells (non - insulin sensitive) ‚Ä¢ Ion passage in nerve cells Tortora : ‚ÄúFacilitated diffusion is a saturable, protein - dependent process.‚Äù c) Osmosis: Movement of water through a selectively permeable membrane from low solute concentration to high solute concentration . Mechanism:

Active Transport Requires ATP (Directly or Indirectly) Moves substances against concentration gradient a) Primary Active Transport: Direct use of ATP to move molecules from low to high concentration . Key example: Na ‚Å∫ /K ‚Å∫ - ATPase Pump ‚Ä¢ 3 Na ‚Å∫ ions pumped out ‚Ä¢ 2 K ‚Å∫ ions pumped in ‚Ä¢ Maintains membrane potential and cell volume Other Examples: ‚Ä¢ H ‚Å∫ /K ‚Å∫ pump in stomach ‚Ä¢ Ca¬≤ ‚Å∫ pump in muscle cells Tortora : ‚ÄúActive pumps are critical for nerve impulse transmission and muscle contraction.‚Äù b) Secondary Active Transport (Cotransport): Uses energy stored in ion gradients (from primary transport) to move other molecules. Types: ‚Ä¢ Symport (co - transport) : Same direction o Na ‚Å∫ and glucose into intestines ‚Ä¢ Antiport (counter - transport) : Opposite directions o Na ‚Å∫ in, Ca ¬≤ ‚Å∫ out in cardiac muscle Ross & Wilson : ‚ÄúSecondary transport couples metabolic energy indirectly to molecule movement.‚Äù 3 . SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 33 INTEGRATED CLINICAL CONTEXT Transport Type Physiological Role Clinical Relevance Simple Diffusion Gas exchange (lungs ‚Üí blood) Impaired in pulmonary fibrosis Facilitated Glucose uptake Defective GLUT - 4 ‚Üí insulin resistance Osmosis Water balance in RBCs Hemolysis in hypotonic solutions Active Transport Na ‚Å∫ /K ‚Å∫ gradients, Ca ¬≤ ‚Å∫ removal Digoxin affects Na ‚Å∫ /K ‚Å∫ pump in heart failure Endocytosis Immune cell phagocytosis Inhibited in immunodeficiency Exocytosis Neurotransmission, hormone secretion Blocked in botulinum toxin exposure References ‚Ä¢ Tortora & Derrickson , Principles of Anatomy & Physiology , Ch. 3 ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson , Anatomy & Physiology in Health and Illness , Ch. 2 ‚Ä¢ BD Chaurasia , Handbook of General Anatomy , Transport Physiology ‚Ä¢ OpenStax A&P ‚Ä¢ Visible Body ‚Äì Membrane Transport ‚Ä¢ Microbe Notes Cell Division : Cell division is a fundamental biological process by which a parent cell divides into two or more daughter cells. It is essential for: ‚Ä¢ Growth of multicellular organisms ‚Ä¢ Repair of damaged tissues ‚Ä¢ Replacement of old or dead cells ‚Ä¢ Reproduction in both unicellular and multicellular organisms The two main types of cell division in humans are: I. Mitosis ‚Äì Occurs in somatic (body) cells II. Meiosis ‚Äì Occurs in germ (sex) cells for reproduction TYPES OF CELL DIVISION Type Cells Involved Resulting Cells Genetic Identity Mitosis Somatic (body) 2 Diploid cells Genetically identical Meiosis Germ cells (testes/ovaries) 4 Haploid gametes Genetically diverse I. Mitosis (Equational Division) : Mitosis is the process by which a single diploid parent cell divides into two genetically identical diploid daughter cells . It ensures the maintenance of chromosome number (2n = 46 in humans). It occurs in cells of the skin, liver, bone, muscles, etc., and is crucial for growth, repair, and asexual reproduction . Cell Cycle Phases :

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 34 The cell cycle includes: ‚û¢ Interphase (Preparation Phase ‚Äì not part of mitosis itself) o G ‚ÇÅ phase : Cell growth, protein synthesis o S phase : DNA replication o G ‚ÇÇ phase : Organelle duplication and preparation for mitosis ‚û¢ Mitotic Phase (M phase) : o Karyokinesis : Division of nucleus (includes mitosis stages) o Cytokinesis : Division of cytoplasm Figure 17 : Stages of mitosis. Stages o f Mitosis : a) Prophase ‚Ä¢ Chromatin condenses into visible chromosomes , each with two sister chromatids joined at a centromere ‚Ä¢ Centrioles move to opposite poles ‚Ä¢ Mitotic spindle (microtubules) begins to form ‚Ä¢ Nucleolus disappears ‚Ä¢ Nuclear envelope starts to break down Tortora & Derrickson : "Prophase marks the start of visible chromosomal movement." b) Prometaphase ‚Ä¢ Nuclear envelope completely disintegrates ‚Ä¢ Spindle fibers attach to kinetochore proteins on centromeres ‚Ä¢ Chromosomes begin moving randomly Ross & Wilson : ‚ÄúPrometaphase serves as a checkpoint for proper chromosome attachment.‚Äù c) Metaphase

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 35 ‚Ä¢ Chromosomes align at the metaphase plate (center of the cell) ‚Ä¢ Spindle fibers from both poles are attached to kinetochores BD Chaurasia : ‚ÄúThis stage ensures that sister chromatids are evenly pulled apart.‚Äù d) Anaphase ‚Ä¢ Sister chromatids separate and move to opposite poles ‚Ä¢ Each chromatid is now considered an individual chromosome ‚Ä¢ Cell begins to elongate Tortora : ‚ÄúAnaphase is the shortest but most critical stage, as errors here can lead to aneuploidy.‚Äù e) Telophase ‚Ä¢ Chromosomes arrive at poles and begin to de - condense into chromatin ‚Ä¢ Nuclear envelope and nucleolus re - form in each new nucleus ‚Ä¢ Spindle apparatus disassembles f) Cytokinesis ‚Ä¢ Cytoplasm divides by cleavage furrow in animal cells (or cell plate in plant cells) ‚Ä¢ Two genetically identical daughter cells are formed Mitosis Phase Chart Phase Events Interphase DNA replication, organelle duplication, cell growth Prophase Chromosomes condense, spindle forms, nuclear envelope disintegrates Prometaphase Spindle fibers attach to kinetochores Metaphase Chromosomes align at metaphase plate Anaphase Sister chromatids separate and move to opposite poles Telophase Nuclear envelope reforms, chromatin de - condenses Cytokinesis Cytoplasm splits, forming two daughter cells Functions o f Mitosis ‚Ä¢ Growth : Increases cell number in developing tissues (e.g., bones, muscles) ‚Ä¢ Repair : Heals wounds by replacing damaged cells ‚Ä¢ Replacement : Renews skin, RBCs, epithelial linings ‚Ä¢ Asexual reproduction : In unicellular organisms b) Meiosis : Meiosis is a special type of cell division that occurs only in germ cells (spermatocytes in males and oocytes in females). Unlike mitosis , which produces two genetically identical diploid cells, meiosis reduces the chromosome number by half , resulting in four genetically unique haploid cells (gametes) .

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 36 Purpose: ‚Ä¢ Maintain a constant chromosome number in sexually reproducing species. ‚Ä¢ Introduce genetic variation through crossing over and independent assortment . Where? ‚Ä¢ Males: In testes (spermatogenesis) ‚Ä¢ Females: In ovaries (oogenesis) Features of Meiosis ‚Ä¢ Two successive divisions: Meiosis I (reductional) and Meiosis II (equational) ‚Ä¢ DNA replication: Occurs only once before Meiosis I during the S - phase of interphase. ‚Ä¢ Chromosome number: Reduced from diploid (2n) to haploid (n). ‚Ä¢ Genetic variability: Achieved by crossing over and random assortment . OVERVIEW TABLE Characteristic Meiosis I (Reductional) Meiosis II (Equational) Chromosome number 2n ‚Üí n (reduced) n ‚Üí n (maintained) DNA replication Before Meiosis I (S phase) None before Meiosis II Number of cells formed 2 cells 4 cells (total) Genetic variation Crossing over & independent assortment Further segregation Phases o f Meiosis Interphase (Preceding Meiosis I) ‚Ä¢ G1 phase: Cell growth, preparation for DNA synthesis. ‚Ä¢ S phase: DNA replication ‚Üí each chromosome now has two sister chromatids. ‚Ä¢ G2 phase: Final preparations for division. Meiosis I ‚Äì Reductional Division Purpose: Homologous chromosomes separate, chromosome number reduced by half. 1. Prophase I (Most complex and longest stage) Homologous chromosomes pair and exchange genetic material. Sub - stages of Prophase I: Substage Events Leptotene Chromosomes condense and become visible. Zygotene Homologous chromosomes begin to pair (synapsis) forming tetrads. Pachytene Crossing over occurs between non - sister chromatids (genetic recombination). Diplotene Synaptonemal complex dissolves, homologues start to separate but remain at chiasmata. Diakinesis Maximum chromosome condensation, spindle formation begins. Crossing over in pachytene ensures genetic diversity. 2. Metaphase I

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 37 ‚Ä¢ Tetrads (homologous pairs) align at the metaphase plate . ‚Ä¢ Spindle fibers attach to kinetochores of homologues. 3. Anaphase I ‚Ä¢ Homologous chromosomes (each with two chromatids) are pulled to opposite poles. ‚Ä¢ Sister chromatids remain together at centromeres. 4. Telophase I & Cytokinesis ‚Ä¢ Chromosomes reach poles. ‚Ä¢ Cytokinesis occurs ‚Üí two haploid cells (each still with duplicated chromosomes). ‚Ä¢ Nuclear membranes may reform briefly. End of Meiosis I: Chromosome number is halved (haploid), but chromatids are still duplicated. Meiosis II ‚Äì Equational Division Purpose: Sister chromatids separate, similar to mitosis. 1. Prophase II ‚Ä¢ Chromosomes condense again. ‚Ä¢ New spindle apparatus forms in each haploid cell. 2. Metaphase II ‚Ä¢ Chromosomes (still X - shaped) align individually at metaphase plate. ‚Ä¢ Spindle fibers attach to kinetochores. 3. Anaphase II ‚Ä¢ Centromeres split , sister chromatids are pulled apart to opposite poles. 4. Telophase II & Cytokinesis ‚Ä¢ Chromatids (now individual chromosomes) reach poles. ‚Ä¢ Nuclear envelope reforms, cytokinesis completes. End of Meiosis II: From one original cell, four genetically distinct haploid cells are produced. SIGNIFICANCE OF MEIOSIS Genetic Variation: ‚Ä¢ Crossing over (Prophase I) ‚Ä¢ Independent assortment (Metaphase I)

They constantly sense, interpret, and respond to cues from their environment and neighboring cells. This dynamic exchange of information is referred to as cell communication . This communication happens through various signalling mechanisms , classified based on the proximity of the target cell to the signal - emitting cell. The four principal forms of intracellular signaling are: a) Contact - dependent (Juxtacrine) b) Paracrine c) Synaptic d) Endocrine Each form utilizes ligands (chemical messengers) and receptors , but differs in delivery method , distance , and speed of action. Tortora & Derrickson define cell signaling as "a complex system of communication that governs basic cellular activities and coordinates cell actions." a) Contact - dependent Signaling (Juxtacrine Signaling) Mechanism

Location Examples ‚Ä¢ Embryonic development (e.g., Notch - Delta signaling) ‚Ä¢ Immune response (e.g., T - cell and antigen - presenting cell interaction) ‚Ä¢ Cardiac tissue : Gap junctions coordinate synchronized contraction Function ‚Ä¢ Facilitates highly specific, localized communication . ‚Ä¢ Enables synchronous cell behavior (e.g., in cardiac or smooth muscle). ‚Ä¢ Regulates cell fate decisions during development. b) Paracrine Signaling Mechanism ‚Ä¢ Cells release signaling molecules (e.g., cytokines, growth factors) into the extracellular fluid . ‚Ä¢ These affect neighboring target cells within the same tissue or organ. ‚Ä¢ Signals are usually short - lived due to rapid uptake, degradation, or diffusion limits. Location Examples ‚Ä¢ Local inflammation (e.g., histamine release) ‚Ä¢ Wound healing (e.g., fibroblast growth factors) ‚Ä¢ Organ development (e.g., Sonic Hedgehog pathway) Function ‚Ä¢ Coordinates local tissue responses ‚Ä¢ Controls tissue regeneration , immune cell recruitment , and angiogenesis c) Synaptic Signaling Mechanism ‚Ä¢ Specialized form of paracrine signaling. ‚Ä¢ Neurons transmit electrical impulses that trigger release of neurotransmitters into the synaptic cleft . ‚Ä¢ Neurotransmitters bind to receptors on adjacent neuron or muscle cells.

Tissue Level of Organization : In human biology, a tissue is defined as a group of structurally similar cells and their extracellular matrix that work together to perform a common function. The study of tissues is called histology . During embryonic development, all tissues arise from three germ layers : ‚Ä¢ Ectoderm ‚Ä¢ Mesoderm ‚Ä¢ Endoderm Figure 21: The three germ layers

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 49 From these layers emerge four primary tissue types , forming the structural and functional foundation of all organs and body systems: No. Tissue Type Key Function 1 Epithelial Covers surfaces, lines cavities, forms glands 2 Connective Supports, protects, and binds tissues 3 Muscle Contracts to produce movement 4 Nervous Conducts impulses for communication Tortora & Derrickson note: ‚ÄúUnderstanding the properties of each tissue type is essential to comprehend how organs function individually and collectively.‚Äù Figure 2 2 : Tissue classification. 1 Epithelial Tissue : Epithelial tissue is one of the four fundamental types of tissues in the human body. It forms protective layers on body surfaces, lines internal cavities and organs, and constitutes glands. These tissues are highly cellular , meaning cells are closely packed with minimal extracellular material. Tortora & Derrickson describe epithelium as "a continuous sheet of cells that forms the lining of body surfaces, cavities, and glands, providing both protection and functional specialization." 1.1 Functions o f Epithelial Tissue ‚û¢ Protection ‚Äì against mechanical, chemical, and microbial stress (skin, oral mucosa) ‚û¢ Absorption ‚Äì nutrients and ions (intestines, renal tubules) ‚û¢ Secretion ‚Äì hormones, enzymes, mucus (glands, stomach) ‚û¢ Excretion ‚Äì wastes via kidney tubules ‚û¢ Filtration ‚Äì blood plasma in renal glomeruli ‚û¢ Sensory reception ‚Äì olfactory epithelium, taste buds ‚û¢ Surface movement ‚Äì cilia sweep mucus/debris (respiratory epithelium) 1.2 General Features of Epithelial Tissue

Unlike epithelial tissue, which is cell - dense , connective tissue is composed mostly of a non - living extracellular matrix (ECM) with relatively fewer cells . The nature of the matrix defines its mechanical properties , such as strength, flexibility, or rigidity.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 55 Tortora & Derrickson define connective tissue as ‚Äúa tissue that supports, binds, protects, insulates, and transports substances throughout the body.‚Äù 2.1 General Features of Connective Tissue Feature Description Origin Derived from mesenchyme (embryonic mesoderm) Components Cells + Fibers + Ground substance = Extracellular matrix (ECM) Vascularity Varies: Blood is highly vascular; cartilage is avascular Innervation Mostly innervated (except cartilage) 2.2 Structural Components of Connective Tissue : Connective tissue is distinguished from other tissues primarily by the presence of an abundant extracellular matrix (ECM) . This matrix separates the relatively few connective tissue cells and determines the tissue‚Äôs physical properties , such as rigidity, elasticity, and resistance to compression. The three major structural components of all connective tissues are: ‚û¢ Cells ‚û¢ Fibers ‚û¢ Ground Substance Figure 31 : Structural components of connective tissue . Together, fibers + ground substance form the extracellular matrix (ECM) , which is the functional and mechanical foundation of connective tissues. i. Cells of Connective Tissue : The types of cells vary depending on the connective tissue type (e.g., loose, dense, bone, blood). These cells can be divided into: A. Fixed (Resident) Cells : Stay in the tissue for extended periods; involved in matrix production and maintenance. Cell Type Description & Function Fibroblasts Most abundant cells in connective tissue proper. Secrete collagen, elastin, ground substance. Maintain ECM.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 56 Adipocytes Fat cells that store triglycerides. Provide insulation and energy reserve. Seen in white and brown fat. Mesenchymal stem cells Undifferentiated; give rise to fibroblasts, chondroblasts, osteoblasts during growth and repair. Fixed macrophages Derived from monocytes. Phagocytic cells that clear pathogens and cellular debris. Antigen - presenting. B. Wandering (Mobile) Cells : Move in and out of connective tissue; mostly immune cells. Cell Type Description & Function Mast cells Contain granules with histamine and heparin. Mediate inflammation and allergic responses. Plasma cells Differentiated B lymphocytes that secrete antibodies (immunoglobulins). Free macrophages Move freely through tissue. Part of immune surveillance. Leukocytes (WBCs) Neutrophils, eosinophils, lymphocytes ‚Äì enter tissues during infection/inflammation. NOTE : Different connective tissues have specialized cells: ‚Ä¢ Chondrocytes in cartilage ‚Ä¢ Osteocytes in bone ‚Ä¢ RBCs/WBCs/platelets in blood ii. Fibers o f Connective Tissue : Fibers are long protein polymers embedded in the ECM, produced by fibroblasts (or chondrocytes in cartilage, osteoblasts in bone). They determine tensile strength , elasticity , and structural integrity of the tissue. A. Collagen Fibers ‚Ä¢ Most abundant protein in the body (~25 ‚Äì 30% of total protein). ‚Ä¢ Composed of collagen type I (in tendons, bone, dermis), type II (cartilage), or type III (reticular fibers). ‚Ä¢ Function : Provide tensile strength (resist stretching forces). ‚Ä¢ Appearance in histology: Thick, wavy bundles, pink - stained in H&E. Example : Dense regular CT (tendons) has parallel collagen bundles for unidirectional strength. B. Elastic Fibers ‚Ä¢ Composed of elastin and fibrillin . ‚Ä¢ Allow tissues to stretch and recoil like a rubber band. ‚Ä¢ Found in organs that undergo frequent deformation (e.g., arteries, lungs, skin). ‚Ä¢ Appearance in histology: Thin, branching fibers; special stains (e.g., Verhoeff or Orcein) are used to see them clearly.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 57 Example : Elastic connective tissue in the aorta wall helps maintain blood pressure during diastole. C. Reticular Fibers ‚Ä¢ Composed of type III collagen , arranged in fine, branching meshworks . ‚Ä¢ Provide a delicate scaffold to support soft organs and form the stroma of lymphoid tissues. ‚Ä¢ Function : Support cells in tissues like spleen, lymph nodes, liver, bone marrow. ‚Ä¢ Stained with silver stain (argyrophilic) in histology. Example : Reticular tissue in lymph nodes provides structural support to immune cells. iii. Ground Substance : Ground substance is the amorphous, transparent, gelatinous material that fills the space between cells and fibers. It acts as a molecular sieve , allowing the exchange of nutrients, gases, and wastes between cells and blood. A. Composition ‚Ä¢ Water : Main component ‚Ä¢ Glycosaminoglycans (GAGs) : Highly negatively charged, attract water o e.g., Hyaluronic acid, chondroitin sulfate, heparan sulfate ‚Ä¢ Proteoglycans : Core protein + GAG chains (e.g., aggrecan) ‚Ä¢ Glycoproteins : Fibronectin, laminin (for cell adhesion) B. Functions ‚Ä¢ Hydration : Holds water like a sponge ‚Äî provides resistance to compression ‚Ä¢ Diffusion : Allows nutrient and waste exchange between cells and capillaries ‚Ä¢ Barrier : Slows pathogen spread ‚Ä¢ Adhesion : Anchors cells to ECM (via integrins and glycoproteins) In cartilage, the ground substance is firm and rubbery (due to high GAG content), while in blood, it's fluid (plasma). SUMMARY TABLE ‚Äì STRUCTURAL COMPONENTS Component Key Elements Function Specialized Forms/Cells Cells Fibroblasts, adipocytes, immune cells Matrix production, energy storage, immunity Chondrocytes (cartilage), osteocytes (bone) Fibers Collagen, elastic, reticular Strength, elasticity, structural support Dense regular CT (tendons), elastic CT (aorta) Ground substance GAGs, proteoglycans, glycoproteins Diffusion, hydration, adhesion, compression resistance Cartilage ECM, blood plasma

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 58 2.3 Types of Connective Tissue : i . Connective Tissue Proper : This includes tissues that bind organs and support epithelia , subdivided into loose and dense connective tissues based on fiber density. A. Loose Connective Tissue ( Fewer fibers , more ground substance, highly cellular ) Loose connective tissue is the most widely distributed type of connective tissue in the human body. It is characterized by a loose arrangement of fibers (collagen, elastic, and reticular) embedded in a gel - like ground substance , with abundant cells such as fibroblasts and immune cells. Because of its soft, pliable nature and rich vascular supply, it fills spaces between organs, cushions tissues, provides support, and serves as a medium for nutrient and waste exchange . A. 1. Areolar Connective Tissue Figure 3 2 : Loose (Areolar) connective tissue. ‚Ä¢ Structure : Loosely arranged collagen, elastic, and reticular fibers ; fibroblasts dominate with macrophages, mast cells, and some white blood cells. ‚Ä¢ Function : Cushions organs, supports epithelium, allows diffusion. ‚Ä¢ Location : Papillary dermis, around blood vessels, nerves, and organs; lamina propria of mucous membranes. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Meshwork appearance; visible nuclei of fibroblasts; collagen and elastic fibers scattered. A. 2. Adipose Tissue ‚Ä¢ Structure : Dominated by adipocytes with peripheral nuclei; very little ECM. ‚Ä¢ Function : Energy storage, thermal insulation, protection (shock absorption). ‚Ä¢ Types : o White fat : Found in adults (e.g., hypodermis, omentum).

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 59 o Brown fat : Found in infants; rich in mitochondria for thermogenesis. ‚Ä¢ Location : Subcutaneous layer, around kidneys, eyeballs, abdomen, breasts. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Large clear cells (lipid droplets lost during staining); nucleus pushed to edge. Figure 3 3 : Adipose tissue. A. 3. Reticular Tissue Figure 34 : Reticular connective tissue. ‚Ä¢ Structure : Network of thin reticular fibers (type III collagen) and fibroblasts (reticular cells). ‚Ä¢ Function : Provides soft internal framework for lymphoid organs. ‚Ä¢ Location : Spleen, lymph nodes, bone marrow. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Reticular fibers seen with silver stains; loosely arranged supporting cells. B. Dense Connective Tissue ( More collagen fibers, less ground substance, fewer cells (mostly fibroblasts) ) Dense connective tissue is a specialized form of connective tissue characterized by a high proportion of closely packed collagen fibers with relatively fewer cells and ground substance. It is designed to provide strong structural support and resist tensile forces , making it a key component of tendons, ligaments, and the dermis of the skin.

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 60 Based on the arrangement of fibers, it is classified into dense regular , dense irregular , and elastic connective tissue , each adapted to specific mechanical demands. Figure 3 5 : Dense connective tissue types. B. 1. Dense Regular Connective Tissue ‚Ä¢ Structure : Collagen fibers arranged in parallel bundles; fibroblasts squeezed between fibers. ‚Ä¢ Function : Withstands tensile stress in one direction. ‚Ä¢ Location : Tendons, ligaments, aponeuroses. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Wavy collagen fibers in one direction; nuclei flattened between bundles. B. 2. Dense Irregular Connective Tissue ‚Ä¢ Structure : Irregularly arranged collagen fibers; scattered fibroblasts. ‚Ä¢ Function : Withstands stress in multiple directions. ‚Ä¢ Location : Reticular dermis, fibrous capsules of organs and joints, periosteum, perichondrium. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Densely packed collagen fibers in many directions. B. 3. Elastic Connective Tissue ‚Ä¢ Structure : Abundant elastic fibers; some collagen; fibroblasts between fibers. ‚Ä¢ Function : Allows stretching and recoil. ‚Ä¢ Location : Walls of large arteries (e.g., aorta), vocal cords, bronchial tubes. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Wavy, dark - stained elastic fibers (special stains like Verhoeff's). ii . Specialized Connective Tissue : This group includes tissues with distinct properties and specialized functions , including cartilage , bone , and blood . A. Cartilage ( Avascular tissue with firm but flexible matrix ) ‚Ä¢ Matrix : High in chondroitin sulfate, proteoglycans, and water. ‚Ä¢ Cells : Chondrocytes in lacunae ; chondroblasts in growing cartilage.

A. 1. Hyaline Cartilage ‚Ä¢ Structure : Glossy, homogeneous matrix of type II collagen. ‚Ä¢ Function : Supports and reinforces, cushions joints. ‚Ä¢ Location : Nose, trachea, larynx, costal cartilage, fetal skeleton, articular surfaces. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Glassy ECM with embedded lacunae; chondrocytes in isogenous groups. Figure 3 6 : Hyaline cartilage. A. 2. Elastic Cartilage ‚Ä¢ Structure : Dense network of elastic fibers plus type II collagen. ‚Ä¢ Function : Provides flexible support. ‚Ä¢ Location : External ear (auricle), epiglottis, auditory (Eustachian) tube. ‚Ä¢ Histology : Chondrocytes in lacunae with a dense web of elastic fibers. Figure 37 : Histological structure of elastic cartilage . A. 3. Fibrocartilage ‚Ä¢ Structure : Dense collagen fibers (type I) with rows of chondrocytes. ‚Ä¢ Function : Strong support and resistance to compression.

C. Blood ( Fluid connective tissue ) ‚Ä¢ Matrix : Plasma (90% water + proteins + solutes) ‚Ä¢ Cells : o Erythrocytes (RBCs) : Carry oxygen via hemoglobin o Leukocytes (WBCs) : Immunity (lymphocytes, neutrophils, monocytes, etc.) o Platelets (Thrombocytes) : Involved in clotting ‚Ä¢ Function : Transport gases, nutrients, hormones; immune response; homeostasis. ‚Ä¢ Location : Circulates through cardiovascular system. SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 64 ‚Ä¢ Ross & Wilson , Anatomy and Physiology in Health and Illness ‚Ä¢ OpenStax ‚Äì Connective Tissue ‚Ä¢ Kenhub ‚Äì Types of Connective Tissue ‚Ä¢ Histology Guide 3 Muscular Tissue : Muscular tissue is a specialized tissue type designed to produce movement by contraction of its cellular units, the muscle fibers . This tissue is highly vascularized , excitable , contractile , extensible , and elastic , enabling the body to perform voluntary and involuntary movements . As per Tortora & Derrickson : ‚ÄúMuscle tissue consists of elongated cells, or fibers , that can use ATP to generate force. It produces body movements, maintains posture, and generates heat.‚Äù There are three major types of muscle tissue: ‚û¢ Skeletal Muscle ‚û¢ Cardiac Muscle ‚û¢ Smooth Muscle Figure 41 : Microscopic comparison of skeletal, cardiac, and smooth muscle tissue. 3.1 Skeletal Muscle Tissue : Skeletal muscle tissue is the most abundant type of muscle in the human body, making up about 40 ‚Äì 50% of total body mass in adults. It is voluntary, striated, multinucleated , and is under the control of the somatic nervous system . Main function: Producing conscious, voluntary movements , postural support , and thermogenesis (heat generation through shivering).

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 65 a) Histogenesis (Origin) ‚Ä¢ Embryological origin : Derived from the paraxial mesoderm , specifically the myotome portion of somites . ‚Ä¢ Precursor cells: Myoblasts ‚Üí fuse together to form a syncytium ‚Üí becomes a muscle fiber (hence multinucleated). ‚Ä¢ Remaining undifferentiated cells: Satellite cells ‚Äì responsible for minor regeneration. b) Microscopic Structure o f Skeletal Muscle Tissue : At the microscopic level, skeletal muscle tissue is highly organized and exhibits distinct structural features. This organization allows skeletal muscles to contract efficiently, maintain alignment, and repair to some extent. Figure 4 2 : Skeletal muscle tissue. Microscopically, skeletal muscle tissue displays: ‚Ä¢ Striations (alternating dark and light bands) ‚Ä¢ Multinucleated fibers ‚Ä¢ Peripheral nuclei ‚Ä¢ Highly ordered myofibrils (bundles of actin & myosin filaments) ‚Ä¢ Connective tissue layers that bind and support the fibers i) Muscle Fiber (Muscle Cell) Description: ‚Ä¢ Also called myofiber ‚Ä¢ It is a long, cylindrical, unbranched, multinucleated cell ‚Ä¢ Formed by the fusion of myoblasts during embryogenesis ‚Üí hence a syncytium ‚Ä¢ Each fiber can range from 10 ‚Äì 100 Œºm in diameter , and up to 30 cm in length Histological Features:

SUBJECT: HUMAN ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY - I ( BP101T ) PREPARED BY ‚Äì Mr. Rahul Singh Shaktawat UNIT ‚Äì I Post link - https://drugee.wordpress.com/2025/08/01/unit - i - 14/ 67 ‚Ä¢ Made of thick filaments (myosin) and thin filaments (actin, troponin, tropomyosin) ‚Ä¢ Highly ordered ‚Üí responsible for striated appearance v) Sarcomere : The sarcomere is the basic structural and functional unit of a myofibril , and therefore the primary engine of contraction in skeletal and cardiac muscle tissue. It is the smallest repeating unit in muscle fibers that actively generates force. Defined as: ‚ÄúThe region of a myofibril between two adjacent Z - lines , composed of overlapping thick (myosin) and thin (actin) filaments that produce contraction via a sliding mechanism.‚Äù Structural Components of t he Sarcomere : A sarcomere is ~ 2.2 micrometers long (in resting muscle) and is organized in a precise arrangement of overlapping thick and thin filaments . This structure is what gives skeletal muscle its striated appearance under the microscope. Region Contents Stains As Function / Note Z - line (Z - disc) Anchors thin filaments (actin) and titin Dark line Boundary of a sarcomere; connects adjacent sarcomeres I - band Only actin filaments Light band Shrinks during contraction A - band Entire length of myosin (thick) filaments Dark band Includes H - zone and actin overlap H - zone Only myosin , no actin overlap Pale zone in center of A - band Narrows during contraction M - line Proteins that hold myosin filaments in place Thin central line Stabilizes thick filament alignment Sarcomeres are arranged end - to - end in long chains called myofibrils , which make up the bulk of the muscle fiber. Thick a nd Thin Filaments 1 . Thick Filaments (Myosin) ‚Ä¢ ~ 16 nm diameter ‚Ä¢ Made of hundreds of myosin molecules ‚Ä¢ Each myosin has: o Rod - shaped tail (binds other myosin) o Globular heads (bind actin & hydrolyze ATP) ‚Ä¢ Arranged with heads pointing outward from the M - line Myosin heads form cross - bridges with actin during contraction. 2 . Thin Filaments (Actin) ‚Ä¢ ~ 8 nm diameter

Sarcomere Changes During Contraction : According to the Sliding Filament Theory , sarcomere shortens not by shrinking filaments, but by increasing overlap. Region During Contraction Z - lines Move closer together I - band Becomes narrower H - zone Shrinks or disappears A - band Remains unchanged Sarcomere length Overall shortens ATP and calcium are essential to this sliding mechanism. Excitation - Contraction Coupling and Sarcomere Function a) Initiation: ‚Ä¢ Action potential reaches neuromuscular junction . ‚Ä¢ Acetylcholine (ACh) is released and binds receptors on sarcolemma. ‚Ä¢ Impulse travels through T - tubules to sarcoplasmic reticulum (SR). b) Calcium Release: ‚Ä¢ SR releases Ca¬≤ ‚Å∫ into sarcoplasm. ‚Ä¢ Ca¬≤ ‚Å∫ binds to Troponin - C ‚Üí tropomyosin shifts ‚Üí exposes actin's active sites. c) Cross - Bridge Formation: ‚Ä¢ Myosin head binds actin , forming cross - bridge. d) Power Stroke: ‚Ä¢ Myosin head pivots ‚Üí pulls actin toward M - line. ‚Ä¢ ADP + Pi released.
